fill-rule="evenodd"></path></svg></span></a></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="mb-md gray-dark font--subhead font-xxs" data-qa="timestamp"><div class="display-date">October 5, 2019 at 6:03 PM EDT</div></div><div class="article-body"><div class="teaser-content"><section><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">CLARKSBURG, W.Va. — Four months after Melanie Proctor’s father was buried with military honors for his combat service in Vietnam, she came home to her farm to find an unfamiliar tan SUV in the driveway.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Two federal agents stepped out into the hot sun in August 2018. Proctor, a tax preparer, wondered whether one of her clients was in trouble.</p></div></section></div><div class="remainder-content"><section><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">“We’re here about your father,” the FBI agent said. “We don’t believe he died of natural causes.”</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Flipping open a laptop on her kitchen counter, the agents showed Proctor her dad’s records from the three days he had been hospitalized at the local VA medical center.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">What the line graph showed was alarming.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">In the early morning hours that April, Felix McDermott’s blood sugar had bottomed to dangerous levels. The retired Army sergeant his family knew as “Pap” died the next morning from severe hypoglycemia.</p></div><div><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns dn db-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute z-0" style="left:50%;top:50%">AD</div><div class="relative z-1"><div class=""><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_inline_bb"></wp-ad></div></div></div></div><div><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns db dn-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute z-0" style="left:50%;top:50%">AD</div><div class="relative z-1"><div class=""><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_mob_bigbox"></wp-ad></div></div></div></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Someone had given her father, who was not a diabetic, a deadly injection of insulin, the investigators told Proctor — and he was not the only one.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Multiple veterans had died under similar circumstances on the same ward, and the agents had come to Proctor’s farm in a hamlet 42 miles east of Clarksburg to ask the unthinkable: They wanted to dig up Pap’s body.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Proctor agreed, and her father was one of seven bodies exhumed in an investigation of 11 suspicious deaths at the Louis A. Johnson VA Medical Center, according to a person familiar with the case who, like others, spoke on the condition of anonymity because it is ongoing.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The 14-month inquiry is the latest criminal investigation to engulf the Department of Veterans Affairs, intensifying questions about whether the country’s largest health-care system is doing enough to protect the veterans in its care.</p></div><div><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns dn db-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute z-0" style="left:50%;top:50%">AD</div><div class="relative z-1"><div class=""><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_inline_bb_2"></wp-ad></div></div></div></div><div><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns db dn-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute z-0" style="left:50%;top:50%">AD</div><div class="relative z-1"><div class=""><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_mob_bigbox_2"></wp-ad></div></div></div></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">In August, a former VA pathologist in Fayetteville, Ark., was indicted on three charges of manslaughter after officials say he <a href="https://www.washingtonpost.com/politics/how-veterans-affairs-failed-to-stop-a-pathologist-who-misdiagnosed-3000-cases/2019/08/30/d66fc098-c5b9-11e9-9986-1fb3e4397be4_story.html?tid=lk_inline_manual_16" title="www.washingtonpost.com">misdiagnosed</a> thousands of patients while using drugs or alcohol. He has pleaded not guilty. Meanwhile, in Beckley, W.Va., a former VA doctor is <a href="https://www.va.gov/oig/pubs/press-releases/VAOIG-StatementonBeckleyWV.pdf" title="www.va.gov">under investigation</a> for sexually assaulting as many as 20 of his male patients, according to two people familiar with the case.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy hide-for-print ma-0 mb-md interstitial italic"><a href="https://www.washingtonpost.com/politics/how-veterans-affairs-failed-to-stop-a-pathologist-who-misdiagnosed-3000-cases/2019/08/30/d66fc098-c5b9-11e9-9986-1fb3e4397be4_story.html?tid=lk_interstitial_manual_17">How Veterans Affairs failed to stop a pathologist who misdiagnosed 3,000 cases</a></p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The cascade of inquiries threatens to undermine trust in the long-troubled agency and undercut President Trump’s promises to reform the far-flung system, the foundation of his pitch to veterans as he runs for reelection.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">In Clarksburg, a small Appalachian community four hours west of Washington, hospital officials said they alerted VA leaders as soon as they learned that their medical staff suspected foul play.</p></div><div><div></div></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The deaths from the second half of 2017 through July 2018, initially found to be of natural causes, are now being investigated as homicides. Federal authorities say they have identified a person of interest in the case.</p></div><div><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns dn db-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute z-0" style="left:50%;top:50%">AD</div><div class="relative z-1"><div class=""><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_inline_bb_3"></wp-ad></div></div></div></div><div><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns db dn-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute z-0" style="left:50%;top:50%">AD</div><div class="relative z-1"><div class=""><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_mob_bigbox_3"></wp-ad></div></div></div></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The probe has come to focus on a now-fired hospital employee, a woman who worked the overnight shift as a nursing assistant and left last year, according to people familiar with the case.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The Washington Post is not using the woman’s name because she has not been charged. Through her son, she declined to speak to a reporter who visited her home outside Clarksburg.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The deaths have set off feverish speculation among local residents and dominated conversation during Tuesday night bingo at the local VFW hall.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">“When you have someone who is 80-something years old and they had health issues, you’re thinking, ‘This was their time,’ ” said Proctor, 53. “You don’t question it. None of us had a clue something was wrong.”</p></div><div><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns db dn-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute z-0" style="left:50%;top:50%">AD</div><div class="relative z-1"><div class=""><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_mob_bigbox_4"></wp-ad></div></div></div></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Investigators have identified similarities in nearly a dozen deaths: Elderly patients in private rooms were injected in their abdomen and limbs with insulin the hospital had not ordered — some with multiple shots, according to people familiar with the case. The insulin, which was quickly absorbed, was given late at night when the hospital staff had emptied out. Within hours, the veterans’ blood-sugar levels plummeted.</p></div><div><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns dn db-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute z-0" style="left:50%;top:50%">AD</div><div class="relative z-1"><div class=""><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_inline_bb_4"></wp-ad></div></div></div></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined"><b> </b>The person of interest was assigned to monitor several of the veterans who died in what are known as one-on-one bedside vigils for patients who need extra attention.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Despite these common denominators, the medical staff and those with oversight of hospital procedures were slow to identify a pattern — a failure that could have cost lives, several people familiar with the investigation said.</p></div><div><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns db dn-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute z-0" style="left:50%;top:50%">AD</div><div class="relative z-1"><div class=""><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_mob_bigbox_5"></wp-ad></div></div></div></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The Clarksburg medical center reported 26 deaths from late 2017 through June 2018, according to an internal VA database that tracks mortality rates across the system. The suspicious deaths accounted for close to half of them, according to the data reviewed by The Post.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The case has also brought new scrutiny to VA’s internal controls. The medical surgical ward in Clarksburg, known as 3A, did not have surveillance cameras, according to people familiar with the case. The woman is believed to have had improper access to the medical supply room. The medicine carts on the floor also were routinely left unlocked.</p></div><div><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns dn db-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute z-0" style="left:50%;top:50%">AD</div><div class="relative z-1"><div class=""><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_inline_bb_5"></wp-ad></div></div></div></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Wesley Walls, a hospital spokesman, said the facility has “many protocols in place to safeguard medication.”</p></div><div><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns db dn-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute z-0" style="left:50%;top:50%">AD</div><div class="relative z-1"><div class=""><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_mob_bigbox_6"></wp-ad></div></div></div></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">But lawmakers are still demanding answers.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">“All of us are up in arms,” said Sen. Joe Manchin III (D-W.Va.), describing the reaction of his colleagues on the Senate committee that oversees veterans’ care. He said he is incredulous that hospital leaders in Clarksburg took so long to put the pieces together.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">“You mean to tell me that for nine months you didn’t know what was going on in your hospital?” Manchin said. “Either you didn’t care, or there was a lack of competency.”</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">“You can’t be this removed” from hospital operations, Manchin said. “It’s almost like you don’t give a darn.”</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The senator said he is preparing to call for a “full-blown” Senate investigation into how VA handled the case.</p></div><div><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns dn db-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute z-0" style="left:50%;top:50%">AD</div><div class="relative z-1"><div class=""><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_inline_bb_6"></wp-ad></div></div></div></div><div><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns db dn-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute z-0" style="left:50%;top:50%">AD</div><div class="relative z-1"><div class=""><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_mob_bigbox_7"></wp-ad></div></div></div></div><div><div></div></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Glenn Snider, the medical center director, has said he notified senior leaders in Washington, including the VA inspector general, as soon as his staff alerted him to their concerns.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">“Immediately upon discovering these serious allegations, Louis A. Johnson VA Medical Center leadership brought them to the attention of VA’s independent inspector general while putting safeguards in place to ensure the safety of each and every one of our patients,” Walls said in a statement.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The spokesman declined to provide details but added: “It’s important to note that regulations and protocols can only do so much to protect against criminal activity.”</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">As the inquiry has unfolded, the hospital has required more rigorous tracking of its medications, including their dispensing and who has access to them, according to people familiar with the case.</p></div><div><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns dn db-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute z-0" style="left:50%;top:50%">AD</div><div class="relative z-1"><div class=""><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_inline_bb_7"></wp-ad></div></div></div></div><div><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns db dn-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute z-0" style="left:50%;top:50%">AD</div><div class="relative z-1"><div class=""><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_mob_bigbox_8"></wp-ad></div></div></div></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">VA Inspector General Michael Missal opened an investigation into the deaths in July 2018. The nursing assistant was moved to a dayside paperwork job and interviewed soon after, a person close to the case said. The hospital did not move to fire her for another seven months, the person said, eventually terminating her for lying on her résumé.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">“Our hearts go out to the families affected by these tragic deaths,” VA Secretary Robert Wilkie said in a statement to The Post. He added that the allegations “do not involve any current” hospital employees, bringing “a small measure of accountability to the situation.”</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Wilkie also called on Missal, who was appointed by President Barack Obama, to bring to a close the investigation he is jointly leading with the U.S. attorney for the Northern District of West Virginia, a Republican appointed by Trump.</p></div><div><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns dn db-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute z-0" style="left:50%;top:50%">AD</div><div class="relative z-1"><div class=""><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_inline_bb_8"></wp-ad></div></div></div></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">“More than a year later, the IG is still investigating and Clarksburg-area veterans are still waiting on the justice and answers they deserve,” Wilkie said.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Missal and U.S. Attorney William J. Powell, have said their inquiry is continuing and remains a top priority. They declined to comment.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Three deaths have been ruled homicide by insulin injection, according to people familiar with the investigation. In recent weeks, the Armed Forces deputy medical examiner changed the death certificates of the others from “natural causes” to “undetermined,” according to autopsy reports and two people familiar with the case.</p></div><div><div></div></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">A possible motive in the deaths is still unclear to investigators. But they say they do not believe they were mercy killings. Although the victims — all male — were hospitalized with underlying medical conditions, none was terminally ill.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">“I was hoping against hope it was a mercy thing,” Proctor said. “You can at least wrap your mind around that.”</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The hospital had told McDermott’s family that his aspiration pneumonia was improving with antibiotics and intravenous fluids. They said he would probably be released in a few days once his strength improved, his daughter recalled.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">“They were saying he was doing real good,” she said.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Then her cellphone rang at 1 a.m. April 9. Pap had taken a turn for the worse, the hospital said. His daughter arrived to find him hooked up to an IV bag of dextrose to raise his blood sugar, which had careened to 12 milligrams per deciliter of blood. The normal range in a non-diabetic is 70 to 120, with the low end registering after several hours of not eating.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">“This happens sometimes,” Proctor recalled the staff telling her.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Her father did not recognize her. He died a few minutes before 9 a.m.</p></div><div><h3 class="font--subhead color-gray-darkest ma-0">Mounting deaths</h3></div><div><div></div></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">West Virginia’s fourth-largest city is set among the hills of a rural region dotted with empty coal fields. VA is a major employer in Clarksburg, but the hospital has struggled to recruit and keep health-care workers to treat its aging veterans, data shows.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The 70-bed hospital sits high on a bluff at the edge of town, an imposing building of worn, salmon-colored bricks built in 1950, when the mountain hollows here emptied every morning into the coal fields and now-shuttered glass factories. Today many of the 70,000 veterans it serves struggle with chronic disease, including diabetes, local doctors say.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The first patient afflicted with what would later be understood as sudden, unexplained hypoglycemia died in the second half of 2017.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The deaths piled up. One in January 2018. Another in March. Three more in April, three days apart, including McDermott. Two more in May. Another two in June. Another in early July, on the same day investigators arrived at the hospital.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The hospital staff recorded their apparent causes: Advanced dementia, sepsis, heart attack — unsurprising determinations for an elderly population with chronic conditions, including a small number with diabetes.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">They also had extremely low blood-sugar levels — some cratering as low as 12 milligrams per deciliter.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">A high enough dose of insulin, especially the rapid-acting form, can be fatal for anyone, even with intervention to counteract it.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">It is “very, very rare” for blood sugar to crater so low that it leads to death, said Yogish C. Kudva, an endocrinologist with the Mayo Clinic in Rochester, Minn.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Two months before investigators arrived, on May 7, 2018, a team of inspectors from Missal’s office visited the medical center for an unannounced, week-long spot inspection to conduct a routine review of clinical operations over the past three years.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Their <a href="https://www.va.gov/oig/pubs/VAOIG-18-01136-313.pdf" title="www.va.gov">report</a> issued in October 2018 found general compliance with patient-safety measures, with one exception: The hospital had weak systems for identifying and reporting red flags that could lead to patient harm.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Clarksburg officials told the inspectors that during the past three years, there had been no “sentinel events,” or episodes of possible lapses in care that harmed patients. But the inspectors identified at least one such case.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The watchdog’s office was unaware that three weeks earlier, three patients had died under suspicious circumstances — because no one had identified them, investigators said.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">By mid-2018, a small number of doctors had begun to feel uneasy with the number of patients who had died of sudden hypoglycemia, according to people familiar with the case.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Still, they did not immediately report their concerns.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">“If you’re not looking for sinister things, it’s going to take a while for something like this to come to light,” said a member of the medical staff, describing internal thinking. “It’s the context in which the hypoglycemia was presented. You’re blind until finally there’s a tipping point.”</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The tipping point came in mid-June, after the death of a Korean War veteran named John Hallman, 87, who served in the Navy. Hallman was admitted with liver issues and possible pneumonia. He died the next morning.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">A team of doctors informally discussed the case. They noticed that the overnight staff had checked Hallman’s blood-sugar level. It had dropped to 26.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">“We said, ‘Who checks the blood sugar on a non-diabetic in the middle of the night while they’re trying to sleep?’ ” the medical staffer recalled. “It was like, ‘Oh my gosh, this is not good.’ ”</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The doctors emailed a high-ranking supervisor with their concerns. The supervisor forwarded the email to the local quality team for review instead of directly notifying the inspector general’s office, as required by law, according to people familiar with the case.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">It would be 10 more days before Snider, the medical center’s director, would escalate the case to the inspector general and other VA leaders in Washington — at least nine months after the first death.</p></div><div><h3 class="font--subhead color-gray-darkest ma-0">'Why is he dying?'</h3></div><div><div></div></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The hospital had missed other clues. Clarksburg’s mortality statistics, viewed monthly by the quality staff, showed death rates twice as high as similar hospitals in the VA system for the first two quarters of 2018, data reviewed by The Post shows.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">“You had 11 people die. There was a missed pattern,” said House Veterans’ Affairs Committee Chairman Mark Takano (D-Calif.). “If you’re a manager, you have to understand the warning signs and patterns of behavior.”</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy hide-for-print ma-0 mb-md interstitial italic"><a href="https://www.washingtonpost.com/politics/an-impaired-va-doctor-has-been-charged-in-three-deaths-congress-wants-to-know-more/2019/09/10/d9920336-d3e6-11e9-9343-40db57cf6abd_story.html?tid=lk_interstitial_manual_94">A VA doctor accused of being drunk was charged in 3 deaths. Congress wants to know more.</a></p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Tony O’Dell, a Charleston, W.Va, attorney representing several families in wrongful-death cases against VA, said one patient lived independently and was hospitalized after a fall but was about to be discharged.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">“Most of these guys were getting better,” O’Dell said. “Each one of these deaths was an opportunity to prevent the next one.”</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">When FBI agents and Missal’s staff arrived on the scene, they pored through patient medical records and began interviewing the staff and the person of interest.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">They discovered that she walked the hospital ward late at night with a bedside glucose meter, pricking the edge of patients’ fingers to test their blood sugar. She pricked one patient 12 times in one night, the person on the medical staff recalled.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Another night, she told the nurses’ station that a patient’s blood sugar had dropped and that she was finding juice to bring it back up. At the time, her attention to blood-sugar levels did not raise alarms; with West Virginia’s high rate of diabetes, frequent checks are not unusual.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Investigators have not determined the source of the insulin they believe was used on patients. In some states, including West Virginia, insulin can be purchased without a prescription. At the hospital, the drug, though not a controlled substance, is secured in the medicine room by a coded keypad, according to people familiar with the protocol. However, people familiar with the case said the person of interest obtained access to the room and that medicine carts were often left unlocked.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Determining who may have accessed the insulin has presented a challenge. The hospital uses surveillance cameras in general areas such as waiting rooms, hallways and parking lots, a spokesman said. VA does not require their use in hospital wards and leaves the decision up to individual facilities.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Soon after they arrived, investigators began knocking on the doors of the victims’ families.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">They drove first to Norma Shaw’s 116-acre farm past a closed coal mine west of town. In March 2018, her husband, George, 81, had shown signs of dehydration and was admitted to the VA hospital. His room was next to the nurses’ station.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">At one point, Shaw, a retired Air Force communications specialist, fell in his hospital room. Then a nurse told his family that George’s blood sugar had dropped. “She said there was no explanation,” his widow recalled. He died nine days later after being transferred to hospice care, where he alternated between agitation and lethargy.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The attending doctor urged them to get an autopsy, his family recalled. “We kept asking: Why is he in hospice?” his daughter, Linda Shaw, recalled. “Why is he dying?”</p></div><div><div></div></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">There was no satisfying answer, they said.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Without hesi­ta­tion, Norma Shaw gave the FBI the go-ahead to exhume his body. “I said, ‘Yes, take him,’ ” she recalled. “I just want to know the cause of it.”</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">In January, Shaw’s body was taken to Dover Air Force Base in Delaware for a forensic autopsy generally performed in cases of violent or suspicious deaths.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The federal medical examiner found four insulin injection sites in his arms and right thigh. The autopsy showed a blood-sugar drop to 17 in a span of a few hours, according to the family’s attorney, David Glover.</p></div><div><h3 class="font--subhead color-gray-darkest ma-0">Laid to rest, again</h3></div><div><div></div></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The insulin deaths have set off rumors and unease in a community where the VA medical center is a local institution.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Linda Shaw, an Air Force veteran who suffers from post-traumatic stress disorder, has canceled her therapy appointments at the hospital she says she is afraid to enter.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">“Do I feel comfortable going there?” she said. “Hell no. I do not feel safe there.”</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The hospital — which had honored the person of interest with an award in 2017 — moved to fire her in February after discovering that she lied on her résumé when she was hired in 2014. At the time, she stated she was certified as a nursing assistant, when, in fact, she was not certified, a person close to the investigation said.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">She remained at the hospital, working a day shift until April, when she lost an appeal of her firing.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The seventh autopsy was completed last month. Although Shaw’s, McDermott’s and one other death have been ruled homicides, the evidence of insulin was not strong enough in the other patients to make that determination, a person close to the investigation said.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">All of the veterans have been reinterred with military funerals.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">In October 2018, Felix McDermott’s gray casket was transported in a hearse back to his grave in Westmoreland County Memorial Park in Pennsylvania.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Melanie Proctor, her husband Dale and their daughter Candy paid their respects once again under a cloudy late-fall sky.</p></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">An honor guard played taps and fired a three-volley funeral salute. The soldiers, at Proctor’s request, slipped six shell casings into the flap of an American flag before presenting it to the family: the first three from his first burial, another three from the second.</p></div><div><div></div></div><div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md italic">Alice Crites contributed to this report.</p></div></section></div></div><div
